            "content": "<b>Gс╣Ыha</b>┬ж is used in the singular,<sup>1</sup> or oftener in the plural,<sup>2</sup> to denote the тАШhouseтАЩ of the Vedic Indian. <b>Dama</b> or <b>Dam</b> has the same sense, while <b>Pasty─Б</b> and <b>Harmya</b> denote more especially the home with its surroundings, the family settlement. The house held not only the family, which might be of considerable size, but also the cattle<sup>3</sup> and the sheep<sup>4</sup> at night. It was composed of several rooms, as the use of the plural indicates, and it could be securely shut up.<sup>5</sup> The door (<b>Dv─Бr,</b> <b>Dv─Бra</b>) is often referred to, and from it the house is called <b>Duroс╣Зa.</b> In every house the fire was kept burning.<sup>6</sup> <dvsn n='P'/>Very little is known of the structure of the house. Presumably stone was not used,<sup>7</sup> and houses were, as in Megasthenes' time,<sup>8</sup> built of wood. The hymns of the Atharvaveda<sup>9</sup> give some information about the construction of a house, but the details are extremely obscure, for most of the expressions used do not recur in any context in which their sense is clear. According to Zimmer,<sup>10</sup> four pillars (<b>Upamit</b>) were set up on a good site, and against them beams were leant at an angle as props (<b>Pratimit</b>). The upright pillars were connected by cross beams (<b>Parimit</b>) resting upon them. The roof was formed of ribs of bamboo cane (<i>vaс╣Г┼Ыa</i>),<sup>11</sup> a ridge called <b>Viс╣г┼лvant,</b> and a net (<b>Akс╣гu</b>), which may mean a thatched covering<sup>12</sup> over the bamboo ribs.
